Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
Third Objection: Neither Immanent nor Transcendent
Imam Razi argues that relations are neither immanent nor transcendent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves that relations cannot be described as immanent or transcendent, thereby refuting the opponent's dichotomy.

Core Idea
If relations were immanent or transcendent, absurd consequences follow.

Having established that relations are real, positive attributes, Imam Razi now shows that they cannot fit into the categories of immanence or transcendence. By examining fatherhood, he demonstrates that immanence would force divisibility and transcendence would make it a separate substance—both impossible. This proves that there exists something that is neither immanent nor transcendent, directly refuting the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that Imam Razi examines two possibilities: immanence (inherence) and transcendence (separateness with direction).
2
Understand the argument against immanence
If fatherhood were immanent, it would have to be divided according to the parts of the father (half in half, third in third). This is clearly absurd.
Relations are not divisible like physical parts.
3
Understand the argument against transcendence
If fatherhood were transcendent with direction, it would be an independent substance separate from the father, which is also absurd.
A relation cannot be a standalone entity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, fatherhood (and by extension all relations) is neither immanent nor transcendent. This proves the existence of something that cannot be categorized that way, refuting the opponent's dichotomy.
This is the core of the third objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا نقيم الدلالة على أن الإضافات موجودات في الإعيان ، ثم تبين أنها يمتنع أن تكون محايثة للعالم، أو مباينة عنه بالجهة وذلك يبطل كلامكم . وإنما قلنا: إن الإضافات أعراض موجودات في الأعيان، لأن المعقول من كون الإنسان أبا لغيره ، مغاير لذاته المخصوصة. بدليل: أنه يمكن أن يعقل ذاته مع الذهول عن كونه أبا أو ابنا . والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم . وأيضا : فإنه يمكن ثبوت ذات منفكة عن الأبوة والبنوة . مثل : عيسى عليه السلام . فإنه ما كان أبا لأحد ، ولا ابنا لأحد . والثابت غير ما هو غير ثابت. فمعنى كونه أبا وابنا ، مغاير لذاته المخصوصة . ثم هذا المغاير إما أن يكون وصفا سلبيا أو ثبوتيا . والأول باطل . لأن عدم الأبوة هو الوصف السلبي . والأبوة رافعة له، ورفع العدم وجود . فثبت : أن الأبوة وصف وجودي مغاير لذات الأب، إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه مستحيل أن يقال : الأبوة محايثة لذات الأب . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه قام بنصف الأب نصف الأبوة، وبثلثه ثلث الأبوة : ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ومحال أن يقال : إنها مباينة عن ذات الأب ، مباينة بالجهة والحيز . وإلا لزم كون الأبوة جوهراً قائما بذاته مباينا عن ذات الأب بالجهة . وذلك أيضا محال . فثبت بهذا الدليل وجود موجود لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه محايث للعالم، أو يقال إنه مباين عنه بالجهة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، بطل قولهم .
The third: We establish the proof that relations are existent particulars, then we show that it is impossible for them to be immanent in the world or transcendent from it with respect to direction, and that refutes your statement. And we only say that relations are accidents existing in particulars because the conceived meaning of a man being a father to another is different from his specific essence. By evidence: it is possible to conceive his essence while disregarding his being a father or a son. And the known is other than what is known. Also, it is possible to affirm an essence separate from fatherhood and sonship, such as Jesus, peace be upon him, for he was not a father to anyone nor a son to anyone. And the affirmed is other than what is not affirmed. So the meaning of being a father and a son is different from his specific essence. Then this different thing is either a negative or a positive attribute. The first is invalid because the absence of fatherhood is the negative attribute, and fatherhood removes it, and the removal of non-existence is existence. So it is established that fatherhood is a positive attribute different from the essence of the father. When this is established, we say: It is impossible to say that fatherhood is immanent in the essence of the father, otherwise it would be necessary to say that half of fatherhood subsists in half of the father, and a third in a third of the father, and it is known that that is false. And it is impossible to say that it is transcendent from the essence of the father, transcendent with respect to direction and space, otherwise it would entail that fatherhood is a substance subsisting by itself, transcendent from the essence of the father with respect to direction, and that is also impossible. So it is established by this proof that there exists an existent of which it cannot be said that it is immanent in the world nor that it is transcendent from it with respect to direction. And when this is established, their statement is refuted.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which is a detailed proof that relations (al-idafat) are real existents and that they cannot be described as either immanent or transcendent, thus refuting the opponent's dichotomy. The argument begins by asserting that relations are accidents (a'rad) existing in particulars (a'yan). The example of fatherhood (ubuwwa) is used. Fatherhood is conceived as different from the essence of the father; one can think of the essence without thinking of fatherhood. Also, Jesus had no father or son, showing that essence can exist without these relations. Therefore, fatherhood is a positive attribute (wajudi) because it removes the negative attribute of non-fatherhood, and the removal of non-existence is existence. Once established that fatherhood is a real positive attribute, the argument examines whether it is immanent or transcendent. If immanent, it would have to be divisible according to the parts of the father, which is false. If transcendent, it would be a separate substance, which is also false. Therefore, fatherhood is neither immanent nor transcendent. By analogy, there must exist something of which the same holds, refuting the opponent's claim that all existents are either immanent or transcendent.

Key Terms
محايثة
مباينة
جوهر
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All existents are either immanent (inherent) or transcendent (separate with direction).
Correction: Imam Razi shows that relations are real but neither immanent nor transcendent, refuting the dichotomy.

Checkpoint
What would happen if fatherhood were immanent in the essence of the father?
A) It would be a separate substance
B) It would have to be divisible like the father's body
C) It would be a mental concept
D) It would be identical with the essence

Key Takeaway
Relations are neither immanent nor transcendent, proving that the dichotomy is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'close' to you in times of need, do you inadvertently imagine Him as physically nearby? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink that mental image?
?
If God is neither immanent nor transcendent in the sense of spatial categories, what does that mean for how you should approach Him in worship? Are there any spatial metaphors you need to consciously avoid?
?
Consider a time you felt God was 'far away' because of a sin or hardship. Did that feeling rely on a spatial concept of distance? How can the truth that God is beyond direction transform your understanding of His nearness?

Proof of a Being Neither Immanent nor Separate
Imam Razi proves a being neither immanent nor spatially separate using fatherhood analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the example of fatherhood to demonstrate that God transcends the categories of immanence and spatial separation.

Core Idea
God's relation to the world is neither immanent nor separate, as shown by the analogy of fatherhood.

This lesson presents a concise yet profound proof from Imam Razi. Using the simple example of fatherhood, he argues that God cannot be understood as either 'inside' the world or 'outside' it in a spatial sense. This refutes the limited view that all existents must fit one of these two categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through without pausing. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement, two rejected possibilities, and a conclusion.
The passage is tightly reasoned; each word matters.
2
Understand the fatherhood analogy
Identify how fatherhood relates to the father. The argument tests two possibilities: immanence and spatial separation. See why both lead to absurdity.
Immanence would fragment the attribute; separation would turn it into a substance.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Imam Razi extends the reasoning from fatherhood to God's relation to the world. Conclude that God must be beyond both immanence and spatial separation.
This is a key point in Islamic theology regarding divine transcendence.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The passage ends by stating that the opponents' claim is refuted. Consider who the opponents might be and why this argument is effective.
The refutation is a direct consequence of the established impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقال : الأبوة محايثة لذات الأب . وإلا لزم أن يقال : إنه قام بنصف الأب نصف الأبوة، وبثلثه ثلث الأبوة : ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . ومحال أن يقال : إنها مباينة عن ذات الأب ، مباينة بالجهة والحيز . وإلا لزم كون الأبوة جوهراً قائما بذاته مباينا عن ذات الأب بالجهة . وذلك أيضا محال . فثبت بهذا الدليل وجود موجود لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه محايث للعالم أو يقال إنه مباين عنه بالجهة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، بطل قولهم .
That it is said: Fatherhood is immanent to the essence of the father. Otherwise it would necessitate that it is said: half of the father is characterized by half of fatherhood, and a third by a third of fatherhood. And it is known that this is false. And it is impossible to say that it [fatherhood] is separate from the essence of the father, separate in direction and space. Otherwise it would necessitate that fatherhood is a substance subsisting by itself, separate from the essence of the father in direction. And that is also impossible. So it is established by this proof the existence of an existent of which it cannot be said that it is immanent to the world, nor that it is separate from it in direction. And when this is established, their statement is refuted.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy that God is neither immanent nor spatially separate. The author uses the example of fatherhood, an attribute of the father. If fatherhood were immanent (محايثة) to the father's essence, it would be partially present in each part of the father, which is absurd. If it were separate (مباينة) in direction, it would be an independent substance, also absurd. Therefore, fatherhood is neither immanent nor separate. By analogy, God's relation to the world must be of a different kind—neither immanent nor spatially separate. The conclusion is that there exists a being (God) that transcends such categories, refuting the opponents' claim that all existents must be either immanent or separate.

Key Terms
محايثة
مباينة
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God must be either 'inside' the world (immanent) or 'outside' in a spatial sense (separate like a substance).
Correction: This passage shows that God's relationship to creation is beyond both categories; He is neither immanent nor spatially separate.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in this passage, why can fatherhood not be said to be either immanent or separate from the father's essence?
A) Because it is a mental concept.
B) Because if immanent it would be partially present and if separate it would be a substance.
C) Because fatherhood is not real.
D) Because the father is a substance.

Key Takeaway
God's relationship to the world is neither immanent nor spatially separate; He transcends both categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you picture God, do you unconsciously imagine Him in a specific place or direction? How might this subtle image contradict the truth that He is neither immanent nor spatially separate? What will you do today to train your mind to think of Him beyond these categories?
?
The analogy of fatherhood shows that even a human attribute does not fit simple immanence or separation. How does this reshape your understanding of God’s attributes? In what areas of your life do you still treat God as if He were limited by human-like relations? What concrete step will you take to align your heart with tawheed?
?
If you truly believed that God is not 'inside' you nor 'outside' you in a spatial sense, how would your prayer and reliance on Him change? What is one thought pattern about God’s nearness you need to abandon after reading this passage?

Divine Transcendence

Immanence and Transcendence: Two Judgments, Not One
Imam Razi refutes the claim that immanence and transcendence are a single judgment with a single cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of existents into immanent and transcendent represents two distinct judgments with separate causes, and how this refutes the opponent's objection.

Core Idea
The division into immanent and transcendent is not one judgment but two, each with its own cause.

In this passage, Imam Razi responds to an opponent who argues that being a substance or an accident cannot be the cause of the division because each prevents one side. Razi exposes the fallacy by showing that the division actually conceals two separate judgments with separate causes. By clarifying the logical structure, he defends the rational coherence of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the opponent's claim and Razi's response.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Razi distinguishes two possible meanings of 'the existent is divided into immanent and transcendent.' Identify each meaning and its consequence.
The first meaning concedes two types, the second claims a single judgment of possibility.
3
Trace the causality
Notice how Razi assigns each judgment to its specific cause: immanence caused by being an accident, transcendence caused by being a substance. This is the core logical move.
4
Understand the fallacy
Realise that the opponent's error is treating the summary statement as one judgment, when it actually refers to two distinct judgments. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أنه من الأحكام . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك معللا بخصوص كونه جوهراً أو بخصوص كونه عرضاً ؟ قوله : «لأن كونه جوهراً يمنع من المحايثة ، وكونه عرضاً يمنع من المباينة بالجهة . وما كان علة لقبول الانقسام إلى قسمين يمتنع كونه مانعاً من أحد القسمين» قلنا : ما الذي تريدونه بقولكم : الموجود في الشاهد منقسم إلى المحايث والمباين بالجهة ؟ إن أردتم به : أن الموجود في الشاهد قسمان : أحدهما هو الذي يكون محايثا لغيره - وهو العرض - والثاني : هو الذي يكون مباينا لغيره بالجهة وهو الجوهر - فهذا مسلم. لكنه في الحقيقة إشارة إلى حكمين مختلفين معللين بعلتين مختلفتين . فإن عندنا كون كونه محايثا لغيره معلل بكونه عرضا، وكون القسم الثاني مباينا عن غيره بالجهة معلل بكونه جوهراً . فبطل قولكم : إن خصوص كونه عرضا وجوهراً لا يصلحان لعلية هذا الحكم . وإن أردتم به أن إمكان الانقسام إلى هذين القسمين حكم واحد ، وأنه ثابت في جميع الموجودات التي في الشاهد . فهذا باطل . لأن إمكان الانقسام إلى هذين القسمين، لم يثبت في شيء من الموجودات التي في الشاهد ، فضلا عن أن يثبت في جميعها . لأن كل موجود في الشاهد فهو إما جوهر وإما عرض . فإن كان جوهراً امتنع أن يكون محايثاً لغيره . فلم يكن قابلا لهذا الانقسام ، وإن كان عرضا امتنع أن يكون مبايناً لغيره بالجهة ، فلم يكن قابلا لهذا الانقسام . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الذي قالوه مغالطة . والحاصل : أن هذا المستدل أوهم أن قوله : «الموجود في الشاهد إما أن يكون محايثا لغيره، وإما أن يكون مباينا عنه بالجهة» إشارة إلى حكم واحد . ثم بنى عليه أنه لا يمكن تعليل ذلك الحكم بخصوص كونه جوهراً ولا بخصوص كونه عرضاً . ونحن بينا : أنه إشارة إلى حكمين مختلفين معللين بعلتين مختلفتين
The third question: Assume that it is among the judgments. So why is it not permissible that it be caused by its being specifically a substance (جوهر) or by its being specifically an accident (عرض)? He [the opponent] said: 'Because its being a substance prevents immanence (محايثة), and its being an accident prevents transcendence in direction (مباينة بالجهة). And whatever is a cause for accepting division into two parts cannot be a preventer of one of the two parts.' We say: What do you mean by your statement: 'The existent in the witnessed world is divided into the immanent and the transcendent in direction'? If you mean that the existent in the witnessed world is two types: one is that which is immanent to another—and that is the accident—and the second is that which is transcendent in direction to another—and that is the substance—then this is conceded. But in reality, it is an indication of two different judgments caused by two different causes. For we have that the fact of its being immanent to another is caused by its being an accident, and the fact of the second type being transcendent in direction from another is caused by its being a substance. Thus your statement is invalid: that specifically being an accident and a substance do not suffice as causes for this judgment. And if you mean by it that the possibility of division into these two types is one judgment, and that it is established in all existents in the witnessed world—then this is false. Because the possibility of division into these two types has not been established in any of the existents in the witnessed world, let alone being established in all of them. For every existent in the witnessed world is either a substance or an accident. If it is a substance, it is impossible for it to be immanent to another, so it is not capable of this division. If it is an accident, it is impossible for it to be transcendent in direction from another, so it is not capable of this division. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that what they said is a fallacy. The sum is that this arguer imagined that his statement: 'The existent in the witnessed world is either immanent to another or transcendent in direction from another' is an indication of one judgment. Then he built upon it that this judgment cannot be caused by its being specifically a substance nor by its being specifically an accident. But we have shown that it is an indication of two different judgments caused by two different causes.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The opponent (السؤال الثالث) concedes that the division is a judgment but argues that the specific nature of being a substance or accident should not be the cause of that judgment, because each of those natures would prevent one of the two parts. Specifically, being a substance prevents immanence (since substances are separate in direction) and being an accident prevents transcendence (since accidents inhere). So the opponent claims that the cause of the division cannot be these specifics. Imam Razi responds by clarifying that the opponent is conflating two separate judgments: the judgment 'this thing is immanent' has its own cause (being an accident), and the judgment 'this thing is transcendent' has its own cause (being a substance). The opponent's statement 'the existent is divided into immanent and transcendent' is merely a summary of two distinct facts, not a single judgment. If the opponent meant that the very possibility of being either immanent or transcendent is a single property that applies to all existents, that is false because no existent in the witnessed world is capable of both: a substance cannot be immanent, and an accident cannot be transcendent. Therefore, the opponent's argument is a fallacy because it treats two separate judgments as one.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
محايثة
مباينة بالجهة
انقسام
علة
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because a substance cannot be immanent and an accident cannot be transcendent, that the specific natures prevent both sides of the division.
Correction: Razi clarifies that these natures are precisely the causes for each side: being an accident causes immanence, being a substance causes transcendence. They do not prevent the division but rather produce its two parts separately.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Imam Razi identifies in the opponent's argument?
A) The opponent assumes the division into immanent and transcendent is a single judgment.
B) The opponent uses an invalid syllogism.
C) The opponent denies the existence of substances.
D) The opponent confuses cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
The division into immanent and transcendent is actually two distinct judgments with separate causes, and treating it as one is a fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes reduce God's closeness and His transcendence to a single comfortable concept, avoiding the tension of holding both as distinct realities? How has this affected your worship?
?
When you feel distant from God, do you assume He is only transcendent and not near? Or when you feel His presence, do you forget His majesty? Which aspect of this truth do you selectively ignore, and what will you do today to correct that imbalance?
?
If the division between immanence and transcendence were a single judgment, as the opponent claimed, what would that imply about your relationship with God? Are you willing to accept the complexity of two distinct truths, even when it challenges your intellect?

The Fourth Objection: Exclusive Causation by Origination or Existence
Razi responds to an objection demanding exclusive causation by temporal origination or existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a survey-based argument for exclusive causation is logically weak and how Imam al-Razi defends rational argumentation from this objection.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

This lesson examines a subtle but important objection against the argument for the existence of a necessary cause. The opponent demands proof that the cause must be either temporal origination or existence, basing this on an exhaustive survey. Imam al-Razi's response reveals a critical principle of reasoning: not finding a possibility does not mean it doesn't exist. This principle protects rational theology from being undermined by incomplete induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: concession, question, survey claim, and Razi's critique.
The opponent concedes that specific natures are not the cause.
2
Identify the opponent's demand
The opponent asks why the cause must be either حدوث or وجود and demands evidence for this exclusivity.
The key term is 'حصر' (exclusive enumeration).
3
Understand Razi's counter
Razi says the most that can be offered is a survey (سبر) which found no other category. But he warns that lack of finding does not prove lack of existence.
Principle: عدم الوجدان لا يدل على عدم الوجود.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this objection would undermine any proof based on open-ended divisions. Razi shows why rigorous proof must avoid such weak induction.
This is a key contribution to kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أنه لا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بخصوص كونه جوهرا ، ولا بخصوص كونه عرضا . فلم قلتم : إنه لا بد من تعليله ، إما بالحدوث وإما بالوجود ؟ وما الدليل على هذا الحصر ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : سبرنا وبحثنا فلم نجد قسما آخر ، إلا أنا بينا في الكتب المطولة: أن عدم الوجدان لا يدل على عدم الوجود ، وشرحنا أن هذا السؤال هادم لكل دليل مبنى على تقسيمات منتشرة ، غير منحصرة بين النفي والإثبات .
The fourth question: We concede that it is not possible to cause this judgment by its being specifically a substance, nor by its being specifically an accident. So why do you say that it must be caused, either by temporal origination (حدوث) or by existence (وجود)? And what is the evidence for this exclusivity? The most that can be said in the matter is: we surveyed and investigated and did not find another category. Except that we have shown in the lengthy books that lack of finding does not indicate lack of existence, and we have explained that this question demolishes every proof that is based on widespread divisions that are not confined to negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is another objection (السؤال الرابع). The opponent concedes that the specific natures of substance and accident do not cause the judgment, but then asks: why must the cause be only temporal origination (حدوث) or existence (وجود)? The opponent demands evidence for this exclusive division. Imam Razi's answer is brief: the most that can be said is that we have surveyed all possibilities and found no other cause. However, this survey method (سبر) is not definitive, as lack of finding does not prove lack of existence. Moreover, this objection is destructive to any proof that relies on such open-ended divisions that are not strictly exhaustive between negation and affirmation.

Key Terms
حدوث
وجود
تعليل
حصر
سبر
عدم الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an exhaustive survey (سبر) can definitively prove that only certain causes exist.
Correction: Razi explains that lack of finding does not prove lack of existence, so such surveys are insufficient for exclusive causation.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi respond to the objection that the cause must be either temporal origination or existence?
A) He accepts the objection and changes his argument.
B) He argues that exhaustive survey is conclusive.
C) He says that lack of finding does not prove lack of existence.
D) He denies that temporal origination is a possible cause.

Key Takeaway
The objection's reliance on survey (سبر) is not a valid refutation because absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have been quick to conclude that because you haven't found evidence of Allah's help, it isn't there. How does Razi's principle — that lack of finding does not indicate lack of existence — challenge that assumption? What will you actively seek out in the coming week to counter that assumption?
?
When you encounter a doubt about your faith, do you tend to rely on 'I haven't seen a convincing answer' as proof that no answer exists? After studying this lesson, what will you do differently when a doubt arises?
?
In your daily life, where have you stopped seeking knowledge or closeness to Allah because you didn't find immediate results? Write down one specific area you will recommit to, knowing that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

The Circularity Argument against Causality
Razi presents the first part of the fifth objection: circularity due to sensible indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the division into immanence and transcendence requires sensible perception, which cannot be assumed for God, leading to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Division into immanence and transcendence only applies to sensible things.

The objector now challenges the very foundation of the division: it only applies to things that can be perceived by the senses. Since God is not subject to sensory perception, the division cannot be applied to Him, and arguing otherwise leads to circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part of the fifth objection, where the objector explains that the division depends on sensible indication.
Focus on the phrase 'إشارة حسية' (sensible indication).
2
Identify the condition for division
The objector states that for any two things to be classified as immanent or transcendent, they must be perceptible by the senses. Understand why this condition is crucial.
3
Recognize the circular reasoning
The objector argues that applying this division to God requires proving He is sensible, which is disputed, so the proof depends on the conclusion—circularity. See why this invalidates the argument.
The term 'دور' (circularity) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : سلمنا أن عدم الوجدان ، يدل على عدم الوجود لكن لا نسلم ( قولكم ) : « إنا ما وجدنا لهذا الحكم علة سوى الحدوث والوجود ، وبيانه : من وجهين : الأول : إن من انحتمل أن يقال : المقتضى لقولنا : إن الشيء إما أن يكون محايثا للعالم ، أو مباينا عنه : هو كونه بحيث تصح الإشارة الحسية إليه . وذلك أن كل شيئين تصح الإشارة الحسية إليهما . إما أن تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما عين الإشارة إلى الآخر . وذلك كما في اللون والمتلون وهذا هو المحايثة - وإما أن تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما غير الإشارة إلى الآخر - وهذا هو المباينة بالجهة فثبت : أن المقتضى لقبول هذه القسمة : هو كون الشيء مشارا إليه بحسب الحس. وعلى هذا التقدير ما لم تقيموا الدلالة على أنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، لا يمكن أن يقال : إنه تعالى يجب أن يكون محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه بالجهة . لكن كونه تعالى مشارا إليه بحسب الحس هو مما وقع النزاع فيه، وحينئذ يتوقف صحة الدليل على صحة المطلوب. وذلك يفضى إلى الدور . وهو باطل . الثاني : ( إنه لا شك : أن ما سوى الله تعالى . إما أن يكون محايثا لغيره ، أو مباينا عن غيره بالجهة . ولا شك : أن الله تعالى مخالف لهذين القسمين بحقيقته المخصوصة إذ لو لم يكن مخالفا بحقيقته المخصوصة لمكان إما مثلا للجواهر أو الأعراض . ويلزم منه كونه تعالى محدثا . كما أن الجواهر والأعراض محدثة . وذلك محال . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن الجواهر والأعراض مشتركان فى الأمر الذي به وقعت المخالفة بينهما ، وبين ذات البارى تعالى . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى القبول الانقسام إلى المحايث ( والمباين ) هو ذلك الأمر ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير سقط هذا السؤال . لأنه لا مشترك بين الجواهر والأعراض إلا الحدوث .
The fifth question: We concede that the failure to find indicates non-existence, but we do not accept your statement: 'We have not found for this judgment any cause other than temporal origination and existence.' Its explanation is from two aspects: The first: It is possible that it be said: The determinant for our statement that a thing is either immanent in the world, or transcendent from it, is that it be such that sense-perception of it is possible. That is because any two things that can be perceived by sense: either the indication to one of them is the same as the indication to the other, as in color and the colored object, and this is immanence; or the indication to one is different from the indication to the other, and this is transcendence in direction. So it is established that the determinant for accepting this division is that the thing be a sensibly perceptible thing. On this basis, unless you establish the proof that He is perceptible by sense, it cannot be said that He must be immanent in the world or transcendent from it in direction. But that He is perceptible by sense is what is disputed; then the validity of the proof depends on the validity of the proposition, which leads to circularity, and that is invalid. The second: There is no doubt that everything other than God is either immanent in something else or transcendent from it in direction. And there is no doubt that God is different from these two categories in His specific reality, for if He were not different in His specific reality, He would be either similar to substances or accidents. This would entail that He is originated, just as substances and accidents are originated. That is impossible. Once this is established, we say: There is no doubt that substances and accidents share in the attribute by which the difference between them and the essence of God occurs. So why is it not permissible that the determinant for accepting the division into immanence and transcendence be that attribute? On this basis, this question falls, because there is no commonality among substances and accidents except temporal origination.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection with two arguments. The objector first concedes that the failure to find indicates non-existence (contrary to the previous answer), but still challenges the claim that only two causes exist. The first argument suggests that the very division into immanence (muḥāyatha) and transcendence (mubāyana) is based on being perceptible by the senses (al-ishāra al-ḥissiyya). If so, applying this division to God would require that He be perceptible by sense, which is disputed, leading to circular reasoning (dawr). The second argument: all created things are either immanent or transcendent, but God is different in His specific reality; if He were not different, He would be like originated things. So why cannot the commonality between substances and accidents—namely, that by which they differ from God—be the cause of the division? The answer is that the only commonality is temporal origination (ḥudūth), so the objection collapses because the opponent's alternative reduces to the same cause. Thus, Razi refutes the objection by showing that the proposed alternative leads back to ḥudūth or to circularity.

Key Terms
محايثة
مباينة
إشارة حسية
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division into immanence and transcendence applies to all beings, including God, without any condition.
Correction: The division only applies to things that are perceptible by the senses; God is not perceptible, so the division cannot be applied without circularity.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument claim is the basis for the division into immanence and transcendence?
A) Temporal origination
B) Existence
C) Sensible indication
D) The will of God

Key Takeaway
The division into immanence and transcendence only applies to sensible things; applying it to God leads to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever unconsciously assume that categories from your physical experience (like 'inside' or 'outside') apply to Him? How can you guard against that assumption starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly logical division can be circular if its foundation is not examined. Is there any belief about God that you hold simply because it 'makes sense' to you, without checking whether it is based on Revelation or a sound proof? Will you examine it today?
?
Razi's opponent here concedes 'failure to find indicates non-existence' but still falls into circularity. Have you ever argued for God's attributes using human analogies, only to realize later that your reasoning was circular? What will you do to ensure your understanding of God is rooted in what He has revealed, not your own projections?

The Commonality Argument: All Created Things Share Temporal Origination
Razi presents the second argument and shows it collapses because the shared attribute is temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a shared attribute among created things could be the cause of the division, and why it reduces to temporal origination.

Core Idea
The only commonality among all created things is temporal origination.

The second argument of the fifth objection proposes that the division might be due to some attribute common to substances and accidents. Razi shows that the only such attribute is temporal origination (ḥudūth), which is exactly what the original argument used, thus the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation of the second part of the fifth objection. Note how the objector describes the distinction between God and creation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مخالف لهذين القسمين بحقيقته المخصوصة'.
2
Identify the shared attribute
The objector suggests that substances and accidents share an attribute that distinguishes them from God, and this attribute could be the cause of the division. Understand what this attribute is.
3
See why the argument fails
Razi points out that the only common attribute among substances and accidents is temporal origination. Therefore, the objector's alternative reduces to the same cause, so the objection is invalid.
The term 'حدوث' (temporal origination) is central.

The Impossibility of Spatial Direction for God due to Circularity
Razi argues that asserting spatial direction for God leads to circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that God can be pointed to by sense leads to circular reasoning and why this refutes the need for spatial direction.

Core Idea
Asserting that God is perceptible by sense creates a circular argument, invalidating the proof.

Razi presents a logical refutation: if someone argues that God must be in a direction because He can be pointed to by sense, they are assuming the very point in dispute. This lesson unpacks that circularity and shows why the argument for direction fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the terms 'pointed to by sense' and 'circularity'.
The passage is dense; reading twice may help.
2
Identify the disputed premise
Find the phrase 'that God is pointed to by sense is something disputed'. This is the key premise that the argument assumes without proof.
This premise is what the proof tries to establish but uses as a given.
3
Trace the circularity
Notice the logic: the proof that God must be in a direction depends on the claim that He is perceptible by sense. But that claim is exactly what is in question. Thus the proof depends on itself—a circular argument.
Circularity makes the argument invalid regardless of its other merits.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that this leads to 'circularity, and it is invalid'. Ask yourself: does this refute all arguments for God's direction, or only those based on sense-perception?
This passage is a specific refutation of one type of argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو كون الشيء مشارا إليه بحسب الحس. وعلى هذا التقدير ما لم تقيموا الدلالة على أنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس، لا يمكن أن يقال: إنه تعالى يجب أن يكون محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه بالجهة. لكن كونه تعالى مشارا إليه بحسب الحس هو مما وقع النزاع فيه، وحينئذ يتوقف صحة الدليل على صحة المطلوب. وذلك يفضي إلى الدور. وهو باطل.
It is that a thing is pointed to by sense. On this assumption, unless you establish the evidence that He is pointed to by sense, it cannot be said that God must be either immanent to the world or separate from it by direction. But that God is pointed to by sense is something disputed. Then the validity of the proof depends on the validity of the conclusion. This leads to circularity, and it is invalid.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that assumes God is perceptible by sense. It shows that if one uses such a premise, the argument becomes circular because the premise is actually the conclusion in dispute. The author states that the very claim that God can be pointed to by sense is contested. Therefore the proof ends up relying on what it seeks to prove, which is a logical fallacy. The passage defines the problem clearly: the predicate 'being pointed to by sense' is not established; thus any argument using it is invalid due to circularity.

Key Terms
مشار إليه
بحسب الحس
محايث
مباين
بالجهة
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the circularity is about a different premise, such as God's existence or oneness.
Correction: The circularity specifically concerns the premise that God is perceptible by sense; it is that very claim which the argument assumes but fails to prove.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence of asserting that God is perceptible by sense?
A) It proves God's existence.
B) It leads to circular reasoning.
C) It is the only way to establish direction.
D) It is accepted by all.

Key Takeaway
The argument that God is spatially directed fails because it presupposes what it tries to prove, leading to a circular and invalid reasoning.

God Cannot Be in a Direction Due to Unique Essence
Razi argues that spatial categories do not apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that spatial categories (immanent or separate) do not apply to God because of His unique essence.

Core Idea
God's essence is so unique that spatial concepts don't apply to Him.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a rational argument for God's transcendence beyond direction. He begins from the observation that all contingent beings are either immanent to something else or separate by direction. However, God's essence is fundamentally different from all things, so these categories cannot apply to Him. Let’s explore how he builds this case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a feel for the argument.
Notice the key terms: محايث (immanent), مباين (separate), and حدوث (temporality).
2
Identify the logical steps
The argument proceeds: (1) all creatures are either immanent or separate; (2) God's essence is unique; (3) if God were like creatures, He would be temporal; (4) since temporal beings share temporality, spatial division stems from it; (5) therefore God, being eternal, is not subject to direction.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Razi's argument prevents any attribution of direction to God. How does this uphold divine transcendence?
This connects to the broader Islamic concept of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لا شك أن ما سوى الله تعالى إما أن يكون محايثا لغيره أو مباينا عن غيره بالجهة. ولا شك أن الله تعالى مخالف لهذين القسمين بحقيقته المخصوصة، إذ لو لم يكن مخالفا بحقيقته المخصوصة لكان إما مثلا للجواهر أو الأعراض. ويلزم منه كونه تعالى محدثا كما أن الجواهر والأعراض محدثة. وذلك محال. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لا شك أن الجواهر والأعراض مشتركان في الأمر الذي به وقعت المخالفة بينهما وبين ذات الباري تعالى. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى للقبول الانقسام إلى المحايث والمباين هو ذلك الأمر؟ وعلى هذا التقدير سقط هذا السؤال. لأنه لا مشترك بين الجواهر والأعراض إلا الحدوث.
Second: There is no doubt that everything other than God is either immanent to something else or separate from it by direction. And there is no doubt that God is different from these two categories by His specific essence, for if He were not different by His specific essence, He would be either similar to substances or accidents. And that would necessitate that He is temporal, just as substances and accidents are temporal. And that is impossible. And when this is established, we say: There is no doubt that substances and accidents share in the attribute by which the difference between them and the essence of the Exalted occurs. So why is it not permissible that what necessitates the acceptance of division into immanent and separate is that attribute? On this assumption, this question falls away. For there is no shared attribute between substances and accidents except temporality.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against God being in a direction. It begins from the premise that all creatures are either immanent or separate by direction. God's essence is unique and different from all creatures. If He were not different, He would be like substances or accidents, which are temporal. Since God is eternal, He cannot be like them. The argument then notes that substances and accidents share the attribute of temporality. So if the ability to be divided into immanent and separate is due to temporality, then God, being eternal, does not have that attribute. Thus, the question of whether God is immanent or separate does not apply to Him. The reasoning is that the only common characteristic among created things is temporality, and since God is not temporal, the spatial division does not apply.

Key Terms
محايث
مباين
بحقيقته المخصوصة
محدث
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument denies God has any relation to space entirely, but it actually shows that spatial categories are inapplicable because they are tied to temporality.
Correction: The argument does not claim God is 'nowhere' in a spatial sense; it says the concepts of immanent and separate do not apply to Him due to His unique, eternal essence.

Checkpoint
What is the shared attribute between substances and accidents that would necessitate spatial division?
A) Existence
B) Temporality
C) Direction
D) Essence

Key Takeaway
Spatial categories (immanent/separate) apply only to temporal beings; God's unique eternal essence transcends direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you still subconsciously picture God as being 'up' or 'above' the universe? How does it feel to realize that even that mental image is false? What would it take for you to truly stop imagining God in any direction?
?
If God is not in any direction, then your salah towards the Qibla is not about God ‘looking’ at you from a certain spot. What does that mean for your concentration in prayer? Are you willing to face your heart toward God without any spatial crutch?
?
The only thing that makes creatures spatial is that they are temporal — they began. You are temporal too. Does your awareness of your own temporality humble you? When you feel empty or lost in the world, do you run to God as if He were a ‘place’ to be found, or do you accept that He is beyond all places yet closer to you than yourself?

Razi's Refutation of Temporality as a Cause for Spatial Division
Razi refutes the sixth objection using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that temporality necessitates spatial division, and why the opponent's own reasoning leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Temporality does not force spatial division; the opponent's logic undermines itself.

Razi presents the sixth objection to his argument for divine transcendence. The opponent concedes the exhaustive division but suggests temporality as the cause. Razi dismantles this with two powerful counterarguments, showing that the opponent's reasoning would also invalidate existence as a cause, which they would not accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read both the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, then two refutations.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two refutations
The first refutation addresses the definition of temporality. The second refutation addresses the ignorance-necessity argument.
Each refutation has multiple sub-points.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Focus on the concept of receptivity (qābilīya) and how it applies to both positive and negative attributes.
Razi argues that the division into immanent/separate is about receptivity, not the composite nature of temporality.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Razi gives two objections: (1) ignorance of the cause does not necessitate ignorance of the effect, and (2) if it did, then knowledge of the cause would necessitate knowledge of the effect, which leads to a problem for the opponent.
The second objection is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice the historical note about Ibn Furak and Ibn al-Haysam. This reinforces that the opponent had no answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس: سلمنا الحصر. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المقتضى لهذا الحكم هو الحدوث؟ قوله أولا: «الحدوث ماهية مركبة من العدم والوجود»، قلنا: كل محدث فإنه يصدق عليه كونه قابلا للعدم والوجود. وأيضا: كون الشيء منقسما إلى المحايث والمباين: معناه كونه قابلا للانقسام إلى هذين القسمين. قالقابلية إن كانت صفة وجودية كانت في الموضعين كذلك، وإن كانت عدمية فكذلك. ولا يبعد تحليل عدم بعدم. وقوله ثانيا: «لو كان المقتضى لهذا الحكم هو الحدوث، لكان الجهل بحدوث الشيء يوجب الجهل بهذا الحكم»، قلنا: الكلام عليه من وجهين: الأول: لم قلتم: إن الجهل بالمؤثر يوجب الجهل بالأثر؟ ألا ترى أن جهل الناس بأسباب المرض والصحة لا يوجب جهلهم بحصول المرض والصحة، وجهل نفاة الأعراض بالمعاني الموجبة لتغير أحوال الأجسام يوجب جهلهم بتلك التغيرات، وجهل الدهري بكونه تعالى قادرا على الخلق والتكوين لا يوجب جهله بوجود هذا العالم؟ لا. الثاني: لو كان الجهل بالعلة يوجب الجهل بالمعلول، لكان العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول. وعلى هذا التقدير لو كان المقتضى لكون الموجودين في الشاهد إما متحايثين أو متباينين بالجهة هو الوجود، لزم أن من علم كون الشيء موجودا أن يعلم وجوب كونه إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا له بالجهة. لكن الجمهور الأعظم من أهل التوحيد يعتقدون أنه تعالى موجود، ولا يعلمون أنه تعالى لا بد وأن يكون إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا له. فوجب على هذا المساق أن لا يكون المقتضى لهذا الحكم هو كونه موجودا. وهذا السؤال قد أورده الأستاذ ابن فورك - رحمه الله أصحابنا - على ابن الهيصم، ولم يقدر أن يذكر عنه جوابا سوى أن قال:
The sixth question: We concede the exhaustive division. So why is it not permissible that the determinant of this judgment is temporality? His statement first: 'Temporality is a quiddity composed of non-existence and existence.' We say: Every temporal thing is truly described as being receptive to non-existence and existence. Also, the meaning of a thing being divided into immanent and separate is its being receptive to division into these two categories. Receptivity – if it is a positive attribute, it is likewise in both cases; and if it is a negative attribute, likewise. And it is not far-fetched to analyze a non-existence by a non-existence. His statement second: 'If the determinant of this judgment were temporality, then ignorance of the temporality of a thing would necessitate ignorance of this judgment.' We say: The discussion on this is from two aspects: First: Why do you say that ignorance of the cause necessitates ignorance of the effect? Do you not see that people's ignorance of the causes of illness and health does not necessitate their ignorance of the occurrence of illness and health, and the ignorance of the deniers of accidents of the meanings that necessitate the change of states of bodies does not necessitate their ignorance of those changes, and the ignorance of the materialist that God is powerful over creation and formation does not necessitate his ignorance of the existence of this world? No. Second: If ignorance of the cause necessitates ignorance of the effect, then knowledge of the cause would necessitate knowledge of the effect. On this assumption, if the determinant for existents in the witnessed world being either immanent or separate by direction were existence, it would follow that whoever knows that a thing exists must know that it is necessarily either immanent to the world or separate from it by direction. But the vast majority of the people of tawḥīd believe that He is existent, yet they do not know that He must be either immanent to the world or separate from it. So on this reasoning, the determinant of this judgment cannot be existence. This question was posed by the master Ibn Fūrak – may God have mercy on our companions – against Ibn al-Hayṣam, and he was not able to give an answer except to say:
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection and its refutation. The opponent concedes the exhaustive division (that everything is either immanent or separate) but asks why the determinant cannot be temporality. The refutation has two parts. First, regarding the opponent's claim that temporality is a composite of existence and non-existence, the response is that every temporal entity receives both existence and non-existence, and the division into immanent/separate is about receptivity – whether positive or negative, it holds. The idea that analyzing non-existence by non-existence is not far-fetched. Second, the opponent argues that ignorance of temporality leads to ignorance of the judgment. The refutation provides two objections: (1) Ignorance of the cause does not necessarily imply ignorance of the effect, as illustrated by examples from medicine, denial of accidents, and materialism. (2) If it did, then knowledge of the cause would require knowledge of the effect, but many monotheists know God exists without knowing His spatial relation, so existence cannot be the determinant either. The passage ends by noting that this argument was used by Ibn Furak against Ibn al-Haysam, who had no answer.

محايث
مباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality (being created in time) is a valid cause for a thing to be spatially located or not.
Correction: Razi shows that temporality does not necessitate spatial division; the opponent's own reasoning would also invalidate existence as a cause, which they accept.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection in the refutation, if ignorance of the cause necessitated ignorance of the effect, what would follow?
A) That knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect.
B) That the cause is unknown.
C) That the effect is independent.
D) That the existence of God is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the temporality claim by showing that the opponent's own logic would force unacceptable consequences, such as requiring all monotheists to know God's spatial relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you doubted a truth about Allah because you couldn't explain its cause. How does Razi's example of the materialist—who knows the world exists without knowing God's power—challenge your assumption that you must know the cause to know the effect?
?
The majority of monotheists believe in God's existence without knowing how He relates to space. Do you feel pressured to explain the 'how' of divine transcendence? Where does that pressure come from, and are you willing to accept that some things are beyond rational deduction?
?
Ibn Furak, a great scholar, used this argument and Ibn al-Haysam had no answer. What does that teach you about the limits of human reasoning in theology? Are you ready to humbly admit when your intellect reaches its boundaries, rather than forcing an explanation?

The Shared Cause Objection
Objection that the proof leads to an impossible conclusion via a shared cause argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses the logic of a shared cause to argue that the opponents' reasoning would imply that God could have the possibility of temporal origination, an impossible conclusion.

Core Idea
A shared cause argument forces an impossible conclusion about God.

The ninth objection challenges the validity of the previous proof by showing that the same reasoning applied to other cases leads to an absurd result. In this first aspect, the objector uses the common property of 'correctness of temporal origination' to argue that its cause must be existence, which would then apply to God. This sets up a devastating counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument. Notice it is structured as an objection with a logical sequence.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical structure
The objector presents a chain: all things other than God share a common property; this property requires a shared cause; two candidates are considered; one is eliminated; the other leads to an impossible conclusion.
This is a standard refutation by counterexample.
3
Understand the prior-in-rank principle
The objector argues that the possibility of temporal origination is logically prior to actual temporal origination, so it cannot be caused by it. Grasp this premise as it is key to the argument.
Prior in rank means logically or ontologically prior.
4
Trace the conclusion
Since the cause must be existence, and God exists, the possibility of temporal origination would apply to God – which is absurd. Recognize why this conclusion is impossible from a theological standpoint.
The objector uses the opponent's own assumed principles.
5
Reflect on the objection's force
Consider how this objection undermines the original proof. If the same reasoning leads to absurdity, the proof is invalid (manqūḍ).
This is a common method of refutation in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال التاسع : إن ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في صور كثيره ، مع أن النتيجة اللازمة عنه : باطلة قطعا ، وذلك يدل على أن هذا الدليل منقوض . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فهو محدث ، فتكون صحة الحدوث حكما مشتركا بينهما . فنقول : هذه الصحة حكم مشترك فلا بد لها من علة مشتركة. والمشترك إما الحدوث أو الوجود . ولا يمكن أن يكون المقتضى لصحة الحدوث هو الحدوث. لأن صحة الحدوث سابقة على الحدوث بالرتبة ، والسابق بالرتبة على الشيء لا يمكن تعليله بالمتأخر عن الشيء. فثبت : أن صحة الحدوث غير معللة بالحدوث ، فوجب كونها معللة بالوجود . والله تعالى موجود ، فوجب أن يثبت في حقه تعالى صحة الحدوث . وهو محال .
The ninth question: Indeed, what you have mentioned of the proof applies in many cases, while the necessary conclusion of it is absolutely false, and that indicates that this proof is invalid. Its explanation is from [multiple] aspects: The first: Everything other than God is originated (muhdath). So the correctness (sihha) of temporal origination becomes a shared judgment between them. We say: This correctness is a shared judgment, so it must have a shared cause. The shared thing is either temporal origination or existence. It is impossible that that which necessitates the correctness of temporal origination is temporal origination itself, because the correctness of temporal origination is prior in rank to temporal origination, and what is prior in rank to a thing cannot be caused by what is posterior to that thing. So it is established that the correctness of temporal origination is not caused by temporal origination. Therefore it must be caused by existence. And God exists, so the correctness of temporal origination must be established in His case. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the ninth question, but only the first of several aspects is presented. The objector argues that the same proof used by the opponent applies in many cases and leads to a false conclusion, so the proof is invalid (manqūḍ). The first example: all things other than God are originated (muhdath). The property 'correctness of temporal origination' (ṣiḥḥat al-ḥudūth) is common to all originated things. This common property requires a common cause. The commonality could be either temporal origination (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd). But temporal origination cannot be the cause because the 'correctness' or possibility of temporal origination is logically prior to temporal origination itself; the prior cannot be caused by the posterior. Therefore, the cause must be existence. Since God exists, He would also have the correctness/possibility of temporal origination, which is impossible. Thus, the argument that existence is the cause of a judgment would lead to an absurdity, so the argument is flawed. Note: The answer to this objection is not provided in this window.

Key Terms
صحة الحدوث
علة مشتركة
سابق بالرتبة
منقوض
حدوث
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector denies that existence can be a cause.
Correction: The objector does not deny that existence can be a cause; rather, he uses the opponent's own assumption that existence is a cause to derive an absurd conclusion, thereby refuting the opponent's proof.

Checkpoint
Why can't temporal origination (ḥudūth) be the cause of the possibility (ṣiḥḥa) of temporal origination?
A) Because it is the same thing
B) Because the possibility is prior in rank to actuality, and the prior cannot be caused by the posterior
C) Because God is eternal
D) Because it leads to an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The objector uses the shared cause argument to show that the opponent's reasoning would force God to have the possibility of temporal origination, which is impossible, thereby invalidating the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical argument that seems to force an impossible conclusion about God, what is your immediate reaction? Do you feel threatened, challenged, or curious? Examine your heart: is your faith secure enough to engage such arguments honestly, or do you avoid them out of fear?
?
This passage shows that even a seemingly strong proof can have hidden flaws. In your own life, where might you be relying on weak reasoning about God? Are there beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to rigorous scrutiny because you are afraid they might crumble?
?
The shared cause objection is a call to think deeply about the implications of our theological claims. Have you ever made a statement about God's attributes without considering its logical consequences? Today, commit to examining one of your core beliefs about God: write down the claim and trace its logical implications, even if they make you uncomfortable.

The Objection: Existence as a Cause for Division
Razi states the impossibility of division into substance and accident for God, then presents the objectors' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that existence necessitates division into substance and accident, and how the opponents argue that the specificity of essence blocks this division for God.

Core Idea
Existence alone does not guarantee the same divisions for all beings.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents an objection raised by those who argue that every existent must be either a substance or an accident based on its existence. They claim that God's unique essence prevents this division, thus preserving His transcendence. This lesson introduces that objection and its premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the opening premise and the objection carefully. Note the structure: first a statement that division is impossible, then the opponent's claim.
2
Identify the key terms
Notice the terms 'جوهر' (substance), 'عرض' (accident), 'وجود' (existence), and 'ماهية' (essence). Understand how these are used in the objection.
The term 'مانع منفك' means a separate impediment.
3
Understand the opponent's logic
The opponents argue that existence is the cause for the division, but a specific essence can block it. Recognize that this is an attempt to exempt God from the division.
This objection relies on the idea of a 'manif' (impediment) that is separate from the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقبل الانقسام إلى الجوهر وإلى العرض. ومعلوم أن ذلك محال. فإن قالوا: إن كل جوهر وعرض فإنه يصح كونه منقسما إلى هذين القسمين نظرا إلى كونه موجودا، وإنما يمتنع ذلك الانقسام نظرا إلى مانع منفك وهو خصوصية ماهيته. قلنا: هذا اعتراف بأنه لا يلزم من كون الوجود علة لصحة أمر من الأمور، أن يصح ذلك الحكم على كل ما كان موصوفا بالوجود، لاحتمال أن تكون ماهيته المخصوصة مانعة من ذلك الحكم. وإذا كان كذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: الوجود وإن اقتضى كون الشيء إما محايثا لغيره وإما مباينا عنه، إلا أن خصوصية ذاته تعالى كانت مانعة من هذا الحكم، فلم يلزم من كونه تعالى موجودا، كونه بحيث يكون إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه بالجهة؟
That it accepts division into substance and accident. And it is known that that is impossible. For if they say: Every substance and accident is validly divided into these two divisions in view of its existence, and this division is prevented only in view of a separate impediment, which is the specificity of its essence. We say: This is an admission that it does not follow from existence being a cause for the validity of a certain matter that that judgment is valid for everything described by existence, because it is possible that its specific essence prevents that judgment. And if that is so, then why is it not permissible to say: Existence, even if it necessitates that something is either immanent in another or separate from it, yet the specificity of His Essence (may He be exalted) prevents this judgment, so it does not follow from His being existent that He is such that He is either immanent in the world or separate from it by direction?
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within a kalam argument about the applicability of the division into substance and accident to God. The opponents claim that every existent is either a substance or an accident due to its existence, but they argue that this division does not apply to God because the specificity of His essence acts as a separate impediment. Razi responds by pointing out that this admission undermines their own position: if existence is the cause of the division, then the specificity of an essence cannot block the judgment unless existence is not a universal cause. He then applies the same reasoning to the division into immanence and separateness relative to the world: God's specific essence could also block that judgment, negating the need for a direction. The argument highlights the principle that causal relationships are not necessarily uniform across all essences.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
وجود
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That every existent must be either a substance or an accident without exception.
Correction: The objection acknowledges that a separate impediment can block the division, so it's not an automatic rule.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what prevents division into substance and accident for God?
A) His non-existence
B) The specificity of His essence
C) His being a substance
D) His being an accident

Key Takeaway
The objection asserts that existence is the cause for division into substance and accident, but a specific essence can block that judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors assume that existence alone is enough to categorize everything. In what areas of your life do you assume that because something exists, it must fit into your preconceived labels? How can you remind yourself that God's essence is beyond such categories?
?
If God's essence is a 'separate impediment' that prevents logical categories from applying to Him, what does that mean for how you approach thinking about God? Are you willing to let go of your neat mental boxes when they do not fit Him?
?
You just read that the impediment is the specific essence of God. Is there any part of your understanding of God that you have reduced to a mere category (like 'just a being' or 'just a creator')? How can you honor His unique essence in your worship and thoughts today?

Razi's Refutation: The Logical Inconsistency Applied
Razi refutes the objection by exposing its logical inconsistency and extends the argument to immanence and separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection by showing its inconsistency and applies the same reasoning to the division into immanence and separation.

Core Idea
If existence is the cause, a specific essence cannot block judgment.

Having presented the objection, Razi now delivers his response. He points out that the opponents' admission—that existence is the cause—undermines their own position. He then turns the tables by applying the same logic to the question of God's relationship to the world: if existence necessitates either immanence or separation, God's essence could also block that judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's response
Read the response carefully. Notice the key phrase 'هذا اعتراف بأنه لا يلزم...' which reveals the flaw in the objection.
2
Analyze the logical move
Razi argues that if existence is the cause, then the specific essence cannot be a separate impediment; otherwise, existence would not be the cause in all cases.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply the reasoning to a new case
Razi then applies the same reasoning to the division into immanence (محايثة) and separation (مباينة). He questions why God's essence could not also block that division.
This shows the objection's flaw leads to an undesirable conclusion for the opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يقبل الانقسام إلى الجوهر وإلى العرض. ومعلوم أن ذلك محال. فإن قالوا: إن كل جوهر وعرض فإنه يصح كونه منقسما إلى هذين القسمين نظرا إلى كونه موجودا، وإنما يمتنع ذلك الانقسام نظرا إلى مانع منفك وهو خصوصية ماهيته. قلنا: هذا اعتراف بأنه لا يلزم من كون الوجود علة لصحة أمر من الأمور، أن يصح ذلك الحكم على كل ما كان موصوفا بالوجود، لاحتمال أن تكون ماهيته المخصوصة مانعة من ذلك الحكم. وإذا كان كذلك، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: الوجود وإن اقتضى كون الشيء إما محايثا لغيره وإما مباينا عنه، إلا أن خصوصية ذاته تعالى كانت مانعة من هذا الحكم، فلم يلزم من كونه تعالى موجودا، كونه بحيث يكون إما محايثا للعالم أو مباينا عنه بالجهة؟
That it accepts division into substance and accident. And it is known that that is impossible. For if they say: Every substance and accident is validly divided into these two divisions in view of its existence, and this division is prevented only in view of a separate impediment, which is the specificity of its essence. We say: This is an admission that it does not follow from existence being a cause for the validity of a certain matter that that judgment is valid for everything described by existence, because it is possible that its specific essence prevents that judgment. And if that is so, then why is it not permissible to say: Existence, even if it necessitates that something is either immanent in another or separate from it, yet the specificity of His Essence (may He be exalted) prevents this judgment, so it does not follow from His being existent that He is such that He is either immanent in the world or separate from it by direction?
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within a kalam argument about the applicability of the division into substance and accident to God. The opponents claim that every existent is either a substance or an accident due to its existence, but they argue that this division does not apply to God because the specificity of His essence acts as a separate impediment. Razi responds by pointing out that this admission undermines their own position: if existence is the cause of the division, then the specificity of an essence cannot block the judgment unless existence is not a universal cause. He then applies the same reasoning to the division into immanence and separateness relative to the world: God's specific essence could also block that judgment, negating the need for a direction. The argument highlights the principle that causal relationships are not necessarily uniform across all essences.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
محايثة
مباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence necessitates all logical divisions uniformly, and that a specific essence can block one division but not another without reason.
Correction: Razi shows that if a specific essence can block the division into substance and accident, it can also block other divisions, so the opponents' argument is self-defeating.

Checkpoint
What does Razi mean by 'this is an admission that it does not follow...'?
A) The opponents have proven their point.
B) The opponents have unintentionally admitted that existence is not a universal cause.
C) Razi agrees with the objection.
D) The opponents have refuted themselves.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that allowing a specific essence to block a judgment undermines the causal claim, and extends the same reasoning to other divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical inconsistency between your understanding of God and a proposed argument, do you truly wrestle with it, or do you quickly dismiss it to avoid discomfort? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to your own reasoning?
?
If you sincerely believe that God's essence is beyond all categories like immanence or separation, how does that affect the way you think about His closeness and distance in your life right now? Are you willing to let go of mental frameworks that try to confine Him?
?
Imagine that you are pressed to defend God's transcendence using logic, but your own argument harbors a hidden inconsistency. Would you rather admit the flaw and search for truth, or defend the flaw because it feels safer? What choice makes you more accountable to God?
